    G. K. Chesterton
   </h1>

        </a>

     <p>
      <b>
       Gilbert Keith Chesterton
      </b>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Order_of_St._Gregory_the_Great" title="Order of St. Gregory the Great">
       KC*SG
      </a>
      (29 May 1874 14 June 1936) better known as
      <b>
       G. K. Chesterton
      </b>
      , was an English writer,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Lay_theologian" title="Lay theologian">
       lay theologian
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Poet" title="Poet">
       poet
      </a>
      , philosopher, dramatist, journalist, orator, literary and art critic, biographer, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_apologist" title="Christian apologist">
       Christian apologist
      </a>
      . Chesterton is often referred to as the "prince of
      <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">
       paradox
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">
        Time
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine, in a review of a biography of Chesterton, observed of his writing style: "Whenever possible Chesterton made his points with popular sayings, proverbs, allegoriesfirst carefully turning them inside out."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Time-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chesterton is well known for his fictional priest-detective
      <a href="/wiki/Father_Brown" title="Father Brown">
       Father Brown
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Father_Brown_on_Chesterton.2C_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Father_Brown_on_Chesterton.2C-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and for his reasoned
      <a href="/wiki/Apologetics" title="Apologetics">
       apologetics
      </a>
      . Even some of those who disagree with him have recognized the wide appeal of such works as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy_(book)" title="Orthodoxy (book)">
        Orthodoxy
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Everlasting_Man" title="The Everlasting Man">
        The Everlasting Man
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Time-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas1974_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas1974-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chesterton, as a political thinker, cast aspersions on both
      <a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">
       Progressivism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">
       Conservatism
      </a>
      , saying, "The whole modern world has divided itself into Conservatives and Progressives. The business of Progressives is to go on making mistakes. The business of the Conservatives is to prevent the mistakes from being corrected."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chesterton routinely referred to himself as an
      <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">
       "orthodox"
      </a>
      Christian, and came to identify this position more and more with Catholicism, eventually converting to
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
       Roman Catholicism
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholicism" title="Anglo-Catholicism">
       High Church
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">
       Anglicanism
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">
       George Bernard Shaw
      </a>
      , Chesterton's "friendly enemy" according to
      <i>
       Time
      </i>
      , said of him, "He was a man of colossal genius."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_3-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Time-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Biographers have identified him as a successor to such Victorian authors as
      <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">
       Matthew Arnold
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">
       Thomas Carlyle
      </a>
      , Cardinal
      <a href="/wiki/John_Henry_Newman" title="John Henry Newman">
       John Henry Newman
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">
       John Ruskin
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>
