     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G.K._Chesterton_at_the_age_of_17.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1167" data-file-width="773" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/G.K._Chesterton_at_the_age_of_17.jpg/220px-G.K._Chesterton_at_the_age_of_17.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/G.K._Chesterton_at_the_age_of_17.jpg/330px-G.K._Chesterton_at_the_age_of_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/G.K._Chesterton_at_the_age_of_17.jpg/440px-G.K._Chesterton_at_the_age_of_17.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:G.K._Chesterton_at_the_age_of_17.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        G. K. Chesterton at the age of 17.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Chesterton was born in
      <a href="/wiki/Campden_Hill" title="Campden Hill">
       Campden Hill
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Kensington" title="Kensington">
       Kensington
      </a>
      , London, the son of Marie Louise (Grosjean) and Edward Chesterton.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He was baptized at the age of one month into the
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">
       Church of England
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though his family themselves were irregularly practising
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Unitarianism" title="Christian Unitarianism">
       Unitarians
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to his autobiography, as a young man Chesterton became fascinated with the
      <a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">
       occult
      </a>
      and, along with his brother
      <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Chesterton" title="Cecil Chesterton">
       Cecil
      </a>
      , experimented with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ouija_boards" title="Ouija boards">
       Ouija boards
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesterton1936.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fgutenberg.net.au.2Febooks13.2F1301201h.html.23ch15_Chapter_IV.5D_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesterton1936.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fgutenberg.net.au.2Febooks13.2F1301201h.html.23ch15_Chapter_IV.5D-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chesterton was educated at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_School_(London)" title="St Paul's School (London)">
       St Paul's School
      </a>
      , then attended the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slade_School_of_Art" title="Slade School of Art">
       Slade School of Art
      </a>
      in order to become an illustrator. The Slade is a department of
      <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">
       University College London
      </a>
      , where Chesterton also took classes in literature, but did not complete a degree in either subject.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Family_life">
       Family life
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Family life">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Chesterton married Frances Blogg in 1901; the marriage lasted the rest of his life. Chesterton credited Frances with leading him back to
      <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">
       Anglicanism
      </a>
      , though he later considered Anglicanism to be a "pale imitation". He entered full communion with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">
       Roman Catholic Church
      </a>
      in 1922.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Career">
       Career
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Career">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In 1896 Chesterton began working for the London publisher Redway, and T. Fisher Unwin, where he remained until 1902. During this period he also undertook his first journalistic work as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freelance" title="Freelance">
       freelance
      </a>
      art and literary critic. In 1902 the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_News_(London)" title="Daily News (London)">
        Daily News
       </a>
      </i>
      gave him a weekly opinion column, followed in 1905 by a weekly column in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_London_News" title="The Illustrated London News">
        The Illustrated London News
       </a>
      </i>
      , for which he continued to write for the next thirty years.
     </p>
     <p>
      Early on Chesterton showed a great interest in and talent for art. He had planned to become an artist and his writing shows a vision that clothed abstract ideas in concrete and memorable images. Even his fiction contained carefully concealed parables.
      <a href="/wiki/Father_Brown" title="Father Brown">
       Father Brown
      </a>
      is perpetually correcting the incorrect vision of the bewildered folks at the scene of the crime and wandering off at the end with the criminal to exercise his priestly role of recognition and repentance. For example, in the story "The Flying Stars", Father Brown entreats the character
      <a href="/wiki/Flambeau_(character)" title="Flambeau (character)">
       Flambeau
      </a>
      to give up his life of crime: "There is still youth and honour and humour in you; don't fancy they will last in that trade. Men may keep a sort of level of good, but no man has ever been able to keep on one level of evil. That road goes down and down. The kind man drinks and turns cruel; the frank man kills and lies about it. Many a man I've known started like you to be an honest outlaw, a merry robber of the rich, and ended stamped into slime."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caricature_of_Chesterton,_by_Beerbohm.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2449" data-file-width="1323" height="407" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Caricature_of_Chesterton%2C_by_Beerbohm.jpg/220px-Caricature_of_Chesterton%2C_by_Beerbohm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Caricature_of_Chesterton%2C_by_Beerbohm.jpg/330px-Caricature_of_Chesterton%2C_by_Beerbohm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Caricature_of_Chesterton%2C_by_Beerbohm.jpg/440px-Caricature_of_Chesterton%2C_by_Beerbohm.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caricature_of_Chesterton,_by_Beerbohm.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">
         Caricature
        </a>
        of Chesterton, by
        <a href="/wiki/Max_Beerbohm" title="Max Beerbohm">
         Max Beerbohm
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Chesterton loved to debate, often engaging in friendly public disputes with such men as
      <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">
       George Bernard Shaw
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">
       H. G. Wells
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">
       Bertrand Russell
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Darrow" title="Clarence Darrow">
       Clarence Darrow
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to his autobiography, he and Shaw played
      <a href="/wiki/Cowboy" title="Cowboy">
       cowboys
      </a>
      in a silent film that was never released.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_wit">
       Visual wit
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Visual wit">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Chesterton was a large man, standing 6feet 4inches (1.93m) and weighing around 286 pounds (130kg). His girth gave rise to a famous anecdote. During the First World War a lady in London asked why he was not "out at the
      <a href="/wiki/Front_(military)" title="Front (military)">
       Front
      </a>
      "; he replied, "If you go round to the side, you will see that I am."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On another occasion he remarked to his friend
      <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">
       George Bernard Shaw
      </a>
      , "To look at you, anyone would think a famine had struck England". Shaw retorted, "To look at you, anyone would think you have caused it".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/P._G._Wodehouse" title="P. G. Wodehouse">
       P. G. Wodehouse
      </a>
      once described a very loud crash as "a sound like G. K. Chesterton falling onto a sheet of tin".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chesterton usually wore a cape and a crumpled hat, with a
      <a href="/wiki/Swordstick" title="Swordstick">
       swordstick
      </a>
      in hand, and a cigar hanging out of his mouth. He had a tendency to forget where he was supposed to be going and miss the train that was supposed to take him there. It is reported that on several occasions he sent a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegram" title="Telegram">
       telegram
      </a>
      to his wife Frances from some distant (and incorrect) location, writing such things as "Am in
      <a href="/wiki/Market_Harborough" title="Market Harborough">
       Market Harborough
      </a>
      . Where ought I to be?" to which she would reply, "Home".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard1944chapter_XV_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard1944chapter_XV-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because of these instances of absent-mindedness and of Chesterton being extremely clumsy as a child, there has been speculation that Chesterton had undiagnosed
      <a href="/wiki/Developmental_coordination_disorder" title="Developmental coordination disorder">
       developmental coordination disorder
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attention_deficit_disorder" title="Attention deficit disorder">
       attention deficit disorder
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Radio">
       Radio
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Radio">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1931, the
      <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">
       BBC
      </a>
      invited Chesterton to give a series of radio talks. He accepted, tentatively at first. However, from 1932 until his death, Chesterton delivered over 40 talks per year. He was allowed (and encouraged) to improvise on the scripts. This allowed his talks to maintain an intimate character, as did the decision to allow his wife and secretary to sit with him during his broadcasts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The talks were very popular. A BBC official remarked, after Chesterton's death, that "in another year or so, he would have become the dominating voice from Broadcasting House."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_veneration">
       Death and veneration
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Death and veneration">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PPXI.jpeg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2447" data-file-width="2525" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/PPXI.jpeg/220px-PPXI.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/PPXI.jpeg/330px-PPXI.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/PPXI.jpeg/440px-PPXI.jpeg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PPXI.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegram" title="Telegram">
         Telegram
        </a>
        sent by Cardinal
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugenio_Pacelli" title="Eugenio Pacelli">
         Eugenio Pacelli
        </a>
        (future Pius XII) on behalf of
        <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XI" title="Pope Pius XI">
         Pope Pius XI
        </a>
        to the people of England following the death of Chesterton.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Chesterton died of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congestive_heart_failure" title="Congestive heart failure">
       congestive heart failure
      </a>
      on the morning of 14 June 1936, at his home in
      <a href="/wiki/Beaconsfield" title="Beaconsfield">
       Beaconsfield
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Buckinghamshire" title="Buckinghamshire">
       Buckinghamshire
      </a>
      . His last known words were a greeting spoken to his wife. The
      <a href="/wiki/Homily" title="Homily">
       homily
      </a>
      at Chesterton's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Requiem_Mass" title="Requiem Mass">
       Requiem Mass
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Cathedral" title="Westminster Cathedral">
       Westminster Cathedral
      </a>
      , London, was delivered by
      <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Knox" title="Ronald Knox">
       Ronald Knox
      </a>
      on 27 June 1936. Knox said, "All of this generation has grown up under Chesterton's influence so completely that we do not even know when we are thinking Chesterton."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He is buried in
      <a href="/wiki/Beaconsfield" title="Beaconsfield">
       Beaconsfield
      </a>
      in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">
       Catholic
      </a>
      Cemetery. Chesterton's estate was
      <a href="/wiki/Probate" title="Probate">
       probated
      </a>
      at 28,389, approximately equivalent in 2012 terms to 1.3 million.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Near the end of his life,
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XI" title="Pope Pius XI">
       Pope Pius XI
      </a>
      invested Chesterton as Knight Commander with Star of the Papal
      <a href="/wiki/Order_of_St._Gregory_the_Great" title="Order of St. Gregory the Great">
       Order of St. Gregory the Great
      </a>
      (KC*SG).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Chesterton Society has proposed that he be
      <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">
       beatified
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He is remembered liturgically on 13 June by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(USA)" title="Episcopal Church (USA)">
       Episcopal Church (USA)
      </a>
      , with a provisional feast day as adopted at the 2009 General Convention.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">
       Writing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Writing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G._K._Chesterton_at_work.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1192" data-file-width="1600" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/G._K._Chesterton_at_work.jpg/220px-G._K._Chesterton_at_work.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/G._K._Chesterton_at_work.jpg/330px-G._K._Chesterton_at_work.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/G._K._Chesterton_at_work.jpg/440px-G._K._Chesterton_at_work.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:G._K._Chesterton_at_work.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Chesterton in his study.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Chesterton wrote around 80 books, several hundred poems, some 200 short stories, 4000
      <a href="/wiki/Essay" title="Essay">
       essays
      </a>
      , and several plays. He was a literary and social critic, historian, playwright, novelist, Catholic theologian
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apologist" title="Apologist">
       apologist
      </a>
      , debater, and mystery writer. He was a columnist for the
      <i>
       Daily News
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_London_News" title="The Illustrated London News">
        The Illustrated London News
       </a>
      </i>
      , and his own paper,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/G._K.%27s_Weekly" title="G. K.'s Weekly">
        G. K.'s Weekly
       </a>
      </i>
      ; he also wrote articles for the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopdia Britannica">
        Encyclopdia Britannica
       </a>
      </i>
      , including the entry on
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">
       Charles Dickens
      </a>
      and part of the entry on
      <a href="/wiki/Humour" title="Humour">
       Humour
      </a>
      in the 14th edition (1929). His best-known character is the priest-detective
      <a href="/wiki/Father_Brown" title="Father Brown">
       Father Brown
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Father_Brown_on_Chesterton.2C_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Father_Brown_on_Chesterton.2C-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      who appeared only in short stories, while
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Was_Thursday" title="The Man Who Was Thursday">
        The Man Who Was Thursday
       </a>
      </i>
      is arguably his best-known novel. He was a convinced Christian long before he was received into the Catholic Church, and Christian themes and symbolism appear in much of his writing. In the United States, his writings on
      <a href="/wiki/Distributism" title="Distributism">
       distributism
      </a>
      were popularized through
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_American_Review_(literary_journal)" title="The American Review (literary journal)">
        The American Review
       </a>
      </i>
      , published by
      <a href="/wiki/Seward_Collins" title="Seward Collins">
       Seward Collins
      </a>
      in New York.
     </p>
     <p>
      Of his nonfiction,
      <i>
       Charles Dickens: A Critical Study
      </i>
      (1906) has received some of the broadest-based praise. According to Ian Ker (
      <i>
       The Catholic Revival in English Literature, 18451961
      </i>
      , 2003), "In Chesterton's eyes Dickens belongs to
      <a href="/wiki/Merry_England" title="Merry England">
       Merry
      </a>
      , not
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">
       Puritan
      </a>
      , England"; Ker treats Chesterton's thought in Chapter 4 of that book as largely growing out of his true appreciation of Dickens, a somewhat shop-soiled property in the view of other literary opinions of the time.
     </p>
     <p>
      Chesterton's writings consistently displayed wit and a sense of humour. He employed paradox, while making serious comments on the world, government, politics, economics, philosophy, theology and many other topics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Views_and_contemporaries">
       Views and contemporaries
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Views and contemporaries">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_acres_and_a_cow.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="291" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Three_acres_and_a_cow.JPG/220px-Three_acres_and_a_cow.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Three_acres_and_a_cow.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Three_acres_and_a_cow.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_acres_and_a_cow.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Self-portrait of Chesterton based on the
        <a href="/wiki/Distributism" title="Distributism">
         distributist
        </a>
        slogan "
        <a href="/wiki/Three_acres_and_a_cow" title="Three acres and a cow">
         Three acres and a cow
        </a>
        ."
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Chesterton's writing has been seen by some analysts as combining two earlier strands in English literature. Dickens' approach is one of these. Another is represented by
      <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">
       Oscar Wilde
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">
       George Bernard Shaw
      </a>
      , whom Chesterton knew well: satirists and social commentators following in the tradition of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_Butler_(1835-1902)" title="Samuel Butler (1835-1902)">
       Samuel Butler
      </a>
      , vigorously wielding paradox as a weapon against complacent acceptance of the conventional view of things.
     </p>
     <p>
      Chesterton's style and thinking were all his own, however, and his conclusions were often opposed to those of
      <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">
       Oscar Wilde
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">
       George Bernard Shaw
      </a>
      . In his book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Heretics_(book)" title="Heretics (book)">
        Heretics
       </a>
      </i>
      , Chesterton has this to say of Wilde: "The same lesson [of the pessimistic pleasure-seeker] was taught by the very powerful and very desolate philosophy of Oscar Wilde. It is the carpe diem religion; but the carpe diem religion is not the religion of happy people, but of very unhappy people. Great joy does not gather the rosebuds while it may; its eyes are fixed on the immortal rose which Dante saw."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesterton1905.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2Fheretics.2Fch7.html_chapter_7.5D_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesterton1905.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2Fheretics.2Fch7.html_chapter_7.5D-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      More briefly, and with a closer approximation of Wilde's own style, he writes in
      <i>
       Orthodoxy
      </i>
      concerning the necessity of making symbolic sacrifices for the gift of creation: "Oscar Wilde said that sunsets were not valued because we could not pay for sunsets. But Oscar Wilde was wrong; we can pay for sunsets. We can pay for them by not being Oscar Wilde."
     </p>
     <p>
      Chesterton and Shaw were famous friends and enjoyed their arguments and discussions. Although rarely in agreement, they both maintained good will toward and respect for each other. However, in his writing, Chesterton expressed himself very plainly on where they differed and why. In
      <i>
       Heretics
      </i>
      he writes of Shaw:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       After belabouring a great many people for a great many years for being unprogressive, Mr. Shaw has discovered, with characteristic sense, that it is very doubtful whether any existing human being with two legs can be progressive at all. Having come to doubt whether humanity can be combined with progress, most people, easily pleased, would have elected to abandon progress and remain with humanity. Mr. Shaw, not being easily pleased, decides to throw over humanity with all its limitations and go in for progress for its own sake. If man, as we know him, is incapable of the philosophy of progress, Mr. Shaw asks, not for a new kind of philosophy, but for a new kind of man. It is rather as if a nurse had tried a rather bitter food for some years on a baby, and on discovering that it was not suitable, should not throw away the food and ask for a new food, but throw the baby out of window, and ask for a new baby.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesterton1905.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2Fheretics.2Fch4.html_chapter_4.5D_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesterton1905.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2Fheretics.2Fch4.html_chapter_4.5D-36">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         36
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Shaw represented the new school of thought,
      <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">
       modernism
      </a>
      , which was rising at the time. Chesterton's views, on the other hand, became increasingly more focused towards the Church. In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy_(book)" title="Orthodoxy (book)">
        Orthodoxy
       </a>
      </i>
      he writes: "The worship of will is the negation of will... If Mr. Bernard Shaw comes up to me and says, 'Will something', that is tantamount to saying, 'I do not mind what you will', and that is tantamount to saying, 'I have no will in the matter.' You cannot admire will in general, because the essence of will is that it is particular."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesterton1905.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2Fheretics.2Fch20.html_chapter_20.5D_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesterton1905.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2Fheretics.2Fch20.html_chapter_20.5D-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Title_Page_of_Orthodoxy,_by_G.K._Chesterton.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1143" data-file-width="743" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Title_Page_of_Orthodoxy%2C_by_G.K._Chesterton.png/220px-Title_Page_of_Orthodoxy%2C_by_G.K._Chesterton.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Title_Page_of_Orthodoxy%2C_by_G.K._Chesterton.png/330px-Title_Page_of_Orthodoxy%2C_by_G.K._Chesterton.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Title_Page_of_Orthodoxy%2C_by_G.K._Chesterton.png/440px-Title_Page_of_Orthodoxy%2C_by_G.K._Chesterton.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Title_Page_of_Orthodoxy,_by_G.K._Chesterton.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Title page of the 1909 edition of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy_(book)" title="Orthodoxy (book)">
          Orthodoxy
         </a>
        </i>
        , first published in the previous year.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      This style of argumentation is what Chesterton refers to as using 'Uncommon Sense'  that is, that the thinkers and popular philosophers of the day, though very clever, were saying things that were nonsensical. This is illustrated again in
      <i>
       Orthodoxy
      </i>
      : "Thus when Mr. H. G. Wells says (as he did somewhere), 'All chairs are quite different', he utters not merely a misstatement, but a contradiction in terms. If all chairs were quite different, you could not call them 'all chairs'."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesterton1908b.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2Forthodoxy.2Fch3.html_chapter_3.5D_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesterton1908b.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2Forthodoxy.2Fch3.html_chapter_3.5D-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Or, again from
      <i>
       Orthodoxy
      </i>
      :
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       The wild worship of lawlessness and the materialist worship of law end in the same void. Nietzsche scales staggering mountains, but he turns up ultimately in Tibet. He sits down beside Tolstoy in the land of nothing and Nirvana. They are both helpless  one because he must not grasp anything, and the other because he must not let go of anything. The Tolstoyan's will is frozen by a Buddhist instinct that all special actions are evil. But the Nietzscheite's will is quite equally frozen by his view that all special actions are good; for if all special actions are good, none of them are special. They stand at the crossroads, and one hates all the roads and the other likes all the roads. The result is  well, some things are not hard to calculate. They stand at the cross-roads.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         39
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Another contemporary and friend from schooldays was
      <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Clerihew_Bentley" title="Edmund Clerihew Bentley">
       Edmund Bentley
      </a>
      , inventor of the
      <a href="/wiki/Clerihew" title="Clerihew">
       clerihew
      </a>
      . Chesterton himself wrote clerihews and illustrated his friend's first published collection of poetry,
      <i>
       Biography for Beginners
      </i>
      (1905), which popularized the clerihew form. Chesterton was also godfather to Bentley's son,
      <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Bentley" title="Nicolas Bentley">
       Nicolas
      </a>
      , and opened his novel
      <i>
       The Man Who Was Thursday
      </i>
      with a poem written to Bentley.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Charges_of_anti-Semitism">
       Charges of anti-Semitism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Charges of anti-Semitism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Chesterton faced accusations of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" title="Anti-Semitism">
       anti-Semitism
      </a>
      during his lifetime, as well as posthumously.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An early supporter of
      <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Dreyfus" title="Alfred Dreyfus">
       Captain Dreyfus
      </a>
      , by 1906 he had turned into an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreyfus_Affair" title="Dreyfus Affair">
       anti-dreyfusard
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From the early 20th century, both his fictional and non-fictional work included caricatures of Jews, stereotyping them as greedy, cowardly, disloyal and communists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In a work of 1917, titled
      <i>
       A Short History of England
      </i>
      , Chesterton considers the royal decree of 1290 by which Edward I
      <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Expulsion" title="Edict of Expulsion">
       expelled Jews from England
      </a>
      , a policy that remained in place until 1655. Chesterton writes that popular perception of Jewish moneylenders could well have led Edward I's subjects to regard him as a "tender father of his people" for "breaking the rule by which the rulers had hitherto fostered their bankers' wealth". He felt that Jews, "a sensitive and highly civilized people" who "were the capitalists of the age, the men with wealth banked ready for use", might legitimately complain that "Christian kings and nobles, and even Christian popes and bishops, used for Christian purposes (such as the Crusades and the cathedrals) the money that could only be accumulated in such mountains by a usury they inconsistently denounced as unchristian; and then, when worse times came, gave up the Jew to the fury of the poor".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_New_Jerusalem_(Chesterton_book)" title="The New Jerusalem (Chesterton book)">
        The New Jerusalem
       </a>
      </i>
      , Chesterton made it clear that he believed that there was a "Jewish Problem" in Europe, in the sense that he believed that Jewish culture (though not Jewish ethnicity) separated itself from the nationalities of Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChesterton1920.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2FNew_Jerusalem.txt_Chapter_12.5D_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChesterton1920.5Bhttp:.2F.2Fwww.cse.dmu.ac.uk.2F.7Emward.2Fgkc.2Fbooks.2FNew_Jerusalem.txt_Chapter_12.5D-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He argued that he was quite in favor of a Jew becoming Prime Minister or Lord Chancellor, under the condition, though, that "every Jew must be dressed like an Arab [...] The point applies to any Jew, and to our own recovery of healthier relations with him. The point is that we should know where we are; and he would know where he is, which is in a foreign land." He suggested the formation of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_homeland" title="Jewish homeland">
       Jewish homeland
      </a>
      as a solution, and was later invited to
      <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">
       Palestine
      </a>
      by Jewish
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zionists" title="Zionists">
       Zionists
      </a>
      who saw him as an ally in their cause. Later he grew out of the notion of Palestine as a Jewish homeland, and suggested somewhere in Africa instead.
     </p>
     <p>
      Chesterton, like Belloc, openly expressed his abhorrence of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hitler" title="Hitler">
       Hitler
      </a>
      's rule almost as soon as it started.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearce2005_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pearce2005-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <i>
       The Truth about the Tribes
      </i>
      Chesterton blasted German race theories writing "the essence of Nazi Nationalism is to preserve the purity of a race in a continent where all races are impure."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The historian Simon Mayers points out that Chesterton wrote in works such as
      <i>
       The Crank
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       The Heresy of Race
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       The Barbarian as Bore
      </i>
      against the concept of racial superiority and critiqued pseudo-scientific race theories saying they were akin to a new religion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <i>
       The Truth About the Tribes
      </i>
      Chesterton wrote "The curse of race religion is that it makes each separate man the sacred image which he worships. His own bones are the sacred relics; his own blood is the
      <a href="/wiki/Januarius#The_Blood_Miracle" title="Januarius">
       blood of St. Januarius
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Mayers records that despite "his hostility towards Nazi antisemitism...[its unfortunate that he made] claims that 'Hitlerism' was a form of Judaism, and that the Jews were partly responsible for race theory."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <i>
       The Judaism of Hitler
      </i>
      Chesterton wrote "Hitlerism is almost entirely of Jewish origin."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <i>
       A Queer Choice
      </i>
      Chesterton maintained that the only possible source of "the Hitlerites" idea of "a Chosen Race" was "from the Jews."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <i>
       The Crank
      </i>
      Chesterton went on to say "If there is one outstanding quality in Hitlerism it is its Hebraism" and "the new Nordic Man has all the worst faults of the worst Jews: jealousy, greed, the mania of conspiracy, and above all, the belief in a Chosen Race."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Mayers also shows that Chesterton didn't just portray Jews as culturally and religiously distinct, but racially as well. Chesterton wrote
      <i>
       The Feud of the Foreigner
      </i>
      in 1920 saying that the Jew "is a foreigner far more remote from us than is a Bavarian from a Frenchman; he is divided by the same type of division as that between us and a Chinaman or a Hindoo. He not only is not, but never was, of the same race."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <i>
       The Everlasting Man
      </i>
      , while writing about human sacrifice, Chesterton indicated that he thought medieval stories about Jews ritually killing Christian children might plausibly have some basis in fact. Chesterton wrote "The Hebrew prophets were perpetually protesting against the Hebrew race relapsing into an idolatry that involved such a war upon children; and it is probable enough that this abominable apostasy from the God of Israel has occasionally appeared in Israel since, in the form of what is called ritual murder; not of course by any representative of the religion of Judaism, but by individual and irresponsible diabolists who did happen to be Jews."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayers85-7_47-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayers85-7-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Everlasting_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Everlasting-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chesterton goes on in the paragraph to speak of "the enormous [devotional] popularity of the Child Martyr of the Middle Ages" and of
      <a href="/wiki/Little_Saint_Hugh_of_Lincoln" title="Little Saint Hugh of Lincoln">
       little St. Hugh
      </a>
      (figures held to have been ritual victims of Jews).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Everlasting_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Everlasting-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In his 1989 biography of Chesterton,
      <i>
       Gilbert: The Man who was G.K. Chesterton
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Coren" title="Michael Coren">
       Michael Coren
      </a>
      quoted the
      <a href="/wiki/Wiener_Library" title="Wiener Library">
       Wiener Library
      </a>
      as having said that they had never thought of Chesterton "as a man who was seriously anti-semitic".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2010, the then director of the Wiener Library denied that either the library or anyone authorised to speak on its behalf had ever issued such statement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In September 2013, following a discussion of the issue on
      <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">
       Twitter
      </a>
      , Coren stated that the disputed quotation had come from a 1985 conversation with a librarian whom he did not name.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although he did not explicitly withdraw his attribution of the quotation to the Wiener Library, neither did he provide any justification for regarding the unnamed librarian's opinions as being those of the library.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Dale_Ahlquist#Gilbert:_The_Magazine_of_the_American_Chesterton_Society" title="Dale Ahlquist">
       American Chesterton Society
      </a>
      has devoted a whole issue of their magazine,
      <i>
       Gilbert
      </i>
      , to defending Chesterton against charges of antisemitism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_Eugenics">
       Opposition to Eugenics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Opposition to Eugenics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In
      <i>
       Eugenics and Other Evils
      </i>
      Chesterton attacked
      <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">
       eugenics
      </a>
      as Britain was moving towards passage of the
      <a href="/wiki/Mental_Deficiency_Act_1913" title="Mental Deficiency Act 1913">
       Mental Deficiency Act 1913
      </a>
      . Some backing the ideas of eugenics called for the government to sterilize people deemed "mentally defective", this view did not gain popularity but the idea of segregating them from the rest of society and thereby preventing them from reproducing did gain traction. These ideas disgusted Chesterton who wrote "It is not only openly said, it is eagerly urged that the aim of the measure is to prevent any person whom these propagandists do not happen to think intelligent from having any wife or children."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eugenics_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eugenics-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He blasted the proposed wording for such measures as being so vague as to apply to anyone, including "Every tramp who is sulk, every labourer who is shy, every rustic who is eccentric, can quite easily be brought under such conditions as were designed for homicidal maniacs. That is the situation; and that is the point...we are already under the Eugenist State; and nothing remains to us but rebellion."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eugenics_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eugenics-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      He derided such ideas as founded on nonsense "as if one had a right to dragoon and enslave one's fellow citizens as a kind of chemical experiment".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eugenics_53-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eugenics-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chesterton also mocked the idea that poverty was a result of bad breeding "[it is a] strange new disposition to regard the poor as a race; as if they were a colony of Japs or Chinese coolies. ...The poor are not a race or even a type. It is senseless to talk about breeding them; for they are not a breed. They are, in cold fact, what Dickens describes: 'a dustbin of individual accidents,' of damaged dignity, and often of damaged gentility."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eugenics_53-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eugenics-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Chesterbelloc">
       The
       <i>
        Chesterbelloc
       </i>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The Chesterbelloc">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shaw,_Belloc_e_Chesterton.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Shaw%2C_Belloc_e_Chesterton.jpg/220px-Shaw%2C_Belloc_e_Chesterton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Shaw%2C_Belloc_e_Chesterton.jpg/330px-Shaw%2C_Belloc_e_Chesterton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Shaw%2C_Belloc_e_Chesterton.jpg/440px-Shaw%2C_Belloc_e_Chesterton.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shaw,_Belloc_e_Chesterton.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">
         George Bernard Shaw
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Hilaire_Belloc" title="Hilaire Belloc">
         Hilaire Belloc
        </a>
        , and G. K. Chesterton.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <i>
        See Also
        <a href="/wiki/G._K.%27s_Weekly" title="G. K.'s Weekly">
         G. K.'s Weekly
        </a>
        .
       </i>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Chesterton is often associated with his close friend, the poet and essayist
      <a href="/wiki/Hilaire_Belloc" title="Hilaire Belloc">
       Hilaire Belloc
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      George Bernard Shaw coined the name
      <i>
       Chesterbelloc
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      for their partnership,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and this stuck. Though they were very different men, they shared many beliefs;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chesterton eventually joined Belloc in the Catholic faith, and both voiced criticisms of
      <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">
       capitalism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">
       socialism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They instead espoused a third way:
      <a href="/wiki/Distributism" title="Distributism">
       distributism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/G._K.%27s_Weekly" title="G. K.'s Weekly">
        G. K.'s Weekly
       </a>
      </i>
      , which occupied much of Chesterton's energy in the last 15 years of his life, was the successor to Belloc's
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Witness" title="New Witness">
        New Witness
       </a>
      </i>
      , taken over from
      <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Chesterton" title="Cecil Chesterton">
       Cecil Chesterton
      </a>
      , Gilbert's brother, who died in World War I.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
       Legacy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Legacy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Literary">
       Literary
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Literary">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Chesterton's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Everlasting_Man" title="The Everlasting Man">
         The Everlasting Man
        </a>
       </i>
       contributed to
       <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">
        C. S. Lewis
       </a>
       's conversion to Christianity. In a letter to
       <a href="/wiki/Sheldon_Vanauken" title="Sheldon Vanauken">
        Sheldon Vanauken
       </a>
       (14 December 1950)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         61
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
          <span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2012)">
           pageneeded
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Lewis calls the book "the best popular apologetic I know",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         62
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       and to Rhonda Bodle he wrote (31 December 1947)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         63
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       "the [very] best popular defence of the full Christian position I know is GK Chesterton's
       <i>
        The Everlasting Man
       </i>
       ". The book was also cited in a list of 10 books that "most shaped his vocational attitude and philosophy of life".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         64
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Chesterton was a very early and outspoken critic of
       <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">
        eugenics
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="//en.wikisource.org/wiki/Eugenics_and_other_Evils" title="s:Eugenics and other Evils">
         Eugenics and Other Evils
        </a>
       </i>
       represents one of the first book length oppositions to the
       <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">
        Eugenics
       </a>
       movement that began to gain momentum in England during the early 1900s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         65
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Chesterton's 1906 biography of
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">
        Charles Dickens
       </a>
       was largely responsible for creating a popular revival for Dickens's work as well as a serious reconsideration of Dickens by scholars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAhlquist2006286_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAhlquist2006286-66">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         66
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_with_Wife_Frances.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="438" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5f/Gilbert_with_Wife_Frances.jpg/220px-Gilbert_with_Wife_Frances.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5f/Gilbert_with_Wife_Frances.jpg/330px-Gilbert_with_Wife_Frances.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5f/Gilbert_with_Wife_Frances.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_with_Wife_Frances.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        G. K. Chesterton with wife Frances.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Chesterton's novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Was_Thursday" title="The Man Who Was Thursday">
         The Man Who Was Thursday
        </a>
       </i>
       inspired the Irish Republican leader
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(Irish_leader)" title="Michael Collins (Irish leader)">
        Michael Collins
       </a>
       with the idea: "If you didn't seem to be hiding nobody hunted you out."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         67
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Collins's favorite work of Chesterton was
       <i>
        The Napoleon of Notting Hill
       </i>
       , and he was "almost fanatically attached to it", according to his friend Sir William Darling who cemented their friendship in their mutual appreciation of Chesterton's work.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         68
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etienne_Gilson" title="Etienne Gilson">
        Etienne Gilson
       </a>
       praised Chesterton's Aquinas volume as follows: "I consider it as being, without possible comparison, the best book ever written on Saint Thomas... the few readers who have spent twenty or thirty years in studying St. Thomas Aquinas, and who, perhaps, have themselves published two or three volumes on the subject, cannot fail to perceive that the so-called 'wit' of Chesterton has put their scholarship to shame."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         69
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Chesterton's
       <a class="extiw" href="//en.wikisource.org/wiki/Page:1909_sep_18_gk_chesterton002.jpg" title="s:Page:1909 sep 18 gk chesterton002.jpg">
        column
       </a>
       in the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illustrated_London_News" title="Illustrated London News">
         Illustrated London News
        </a>
       </i>
       on 18 September 1909 had a profound effect on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi" title="Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi">
        Mahatma Gandhi
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         70
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/P._N._Furbank" title="P. N. Furbank">
        P. N. Furbank
       </a>
       asserts that Gandhi was "thunderstruck" when he read it,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         71
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       while Martin Green notes that "Gandhi was so delighted with this that he told
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Indian_Opinion" title="Indian Opinion">
         Indian Opinion
        </a>
       </i>
       to reprint it."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         72
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Archbishop
       <a href="/wiki/Fulton_J._Sheen" title="Fulton J. Sheen">
        Fulton J. Sheen
       </a>
       , author of seventy books, identified Chesterton as the stylist who had the greatest impact on his own writing, stating in his autobiography
       <i>
        Treasure in Clay
       </i>
       "The greatest influence in writing was G. K. Chesterton who never used a useless word, who saw the value of a paradox, and avoided what was trite."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         73
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Chesterton wrote the introduction for Sheen's book
       <i>
        God and Intelligence in Modern Philosophy; A Critical Study in the Light of the Philosophy of Saint Thomas
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         74
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Canadian Media Guru
       <a href="/wiki/Marshall_McLuhan" title="Marshall McLuhan">
        Marshall McLuhan
       </a>
       was heavily influenced by Chesterton; McLuhan said the book
       <i>
        What's Wrong with the World
       </i>
       changed his life in terms of ideas and religion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         75
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gaiman" title="Neil Gaiman">
        Neil Gaiman
       </a>
       has stated that he grew up reading Chesterton in his school's library, and that
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Napoleon_of_Notting_Hill" title="The Napoleon of Notting Hill">
         The Napoleon of Notting Hill
        </a>
       </i>
       was an important influence on his own book
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Neverwhere" title="Neverwhere">
         Neverwhere
        </a>
       </i>
       , which used a quote from it as an epigraph. Gaiman also based the character
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Characters_of_The_Sandman#Fiddler.27s_Green" title="Characters of The Sandman">
        Gilbert
       </a>
       , from the comic book
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Sandman_(DC_Comics_Modern_Age)" title="The Sandman (DC Comics Modern Age)">
         The Sandman
        </a>
       </i>
       , on Chesterton,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         76
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Good_Omens" title="Good Omens">
        novel
       </a>
       he co-wrote with
       <a href="/wiki/Terry_Pratchett" title="Terry Pratchett">
        Terry Pratchett
       </a>
       is dedicated to him.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argentine" title="Argentine">
        Argentine
       </a>
       author and essayist
       <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">
        Jorge Luis Borges
       </a>
       cited Chesterton as a major influence on his own fiction. In an interview with
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burgin_(writer)" title="Richard Burgin (writer)">
        Richard Burgin
       </a>
       during the late 1960s, Borges said, "Chesterton knew how to make the most of a detective story."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         77
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Chesterton's fence
       </b>
       is the principle that reforms should not be made until the reasoning behind the existing state of affairs is understood. The quotation is from Chestertons 1929 book
       <i>
        The Thing: Why I am a Catholic
       </i>
       , in the chapter entitled "The Drift from Domesticity": "In the matter of reforming things, as distinct from deforming them, there is one plain and simple principle; a principle which will probably be called a paradox. There exists in such a case a certain institution or law; let us say, for the sake of simplicity, a fence or gate erected across a road. The more modern type of reformer goes gaily up to it and says, I dont see the use of this; let us clear it away." To which the more intelligent type of reformer will do well to answer: "If you dont see the use of it, I certainly wont let you clear it away. Go away and think. Then, when you can come back and tell me that you do see the use of it, I may allow you to destroy it."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         78
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other">
       Other
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=G._K._Chesterton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1974, Father Ian Boyd, C.S.B, founded The Chesterton Review, a scholarly journal devoted to Chesterton and his circle. The journal is published by the G.K. Chesterton Institute for Faith and Culture based in Seton Hall University, South Orange, U.S.A.
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Dale_Ahlquist" title="Dale Ahlquist">
        Dale Ahlquist
       </a>
       founded the American Chesterton Society in 1996 to explore and promote his writings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         79
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2008, a
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic
       </a>
       high school, Chesterton Academy, opened in the
       <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">
        Minneapolis
       </a>
       area.
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2012, a crater on the planet
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       was named Chesterton after the author.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         80
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In the Fall of 2014, a
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic
       </a>
       high school, G.K. Chesterton Academy of Chicago, is set to open in
       <a href="/wiki/Highland_Park,_Illinois" title="Highland Park, Illinois">
        Highland Park, Illinois
       </a>
       , a suburb of
       <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">
        Chicago
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         81
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       A fictionalized GK Chesterton is the central character in the
       <i>
        Young Chesterton Chronicles
       </i>
       , a series of
       <a href="/wiki/Young-adult_fiction" title="Young-adult fiction">
        young adult
       </a>
       adventure novels written by John McNichol, and published by
       <a href="/wiki/Sophia_Institute_Press" title="Sophia Institute Press">
        Sophia Institute Press
       </a>
       and Bezalel Books.
      </li>
      <li>
       A fictionalized GK Chesterton is the central character in the
       <i>
        G K Chesterton Mystery series
       </i>
       , a series of detective novels written by Australian
       <a href="/wiki/Kel_Richards" title="Kel Richards">
        Kel Richards
       </a>
       , and published by Riveroak Publishing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         82
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Chesterton wrote the hymn
       <i>
        O God of Earth and Altar
       </i>
       which was printed in
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Social_Union_(Church_of_England)#Development" title="Christian Social Union (Church of England)">
         The Commonwealth
        </a>
       </i>
       and then included in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/English_Hymnal" title="English Hymnal">
         English Hymnal
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1906.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         83
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Several lines of the hymn are sung in the beginning of the song
       <i>
        Revelations
       </i>
       by the British heavy metal band
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">
        Iron Maiden
       </a>
       on their 1983 album
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Piece_of_Mind" title="Piece of Mind">
         Piece of Mind
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         84
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Lead singer
       <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Dickinson" title="Bruce Dickinson">
        Bruce Dickinson
       </a>
       in an interview stated "I have a fondness for hymns. I love some of the ritual, the beautiful words,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/And_did_those_feet_in_ancient_time" title="And did those feet in ancient time">
         Jerusalem
        </a>
       </i>
       and there was another one, with words by G.K. Chesterton
       <i>
        O God of Earth and Altar
       </i>
       - very fire and brimstone: 'Bow down and hear our cry'. I used that for an Iron Maiden song,
       <i>
        Revelations
       </i>
       . In my strange and clumsy way I was trying to say look its all the same stuff."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         85
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>

        <i>
         The Autobiography
        </i>

       </li>
